class="mw-indicators mw-body-content">\n</div>\n<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">West Midlands (region)</h1>\t\t\t<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">\n\t\t\t\t<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>\t\t\t\t<div id="contentSub"></div>\n\t\t\t\t<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>\t\t\t\t<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>\n\t\t\t\t<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>\n\t\t\t\t<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><p class="mw-empty-elt">\n</p>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Not to be confused with <a href="/wiki/West_Midlands_(county)" title="West Midlands (county)">West Midlands (county)</a> or <a href="/wiki/West_Midlands_conurbation" title="West Midlands conurbation">West Midlands conurbation</a>.</div>\n<p class="mw-empty-elt">\n</p>\n<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Place in England, United Kingdom</div>\n<table class="infobox geography vcard" style="width:22em;width:23em"><tbody><tr><th colspan="2" style="text-align:center;font-size:125%;font-weight:bold;font-size:1.25em; white-space:nowrap"><span class="fn org">West Midlands</span></th></tr><tr class="mergedtoprow"><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">\n<a href="/wiki/File:West_Midlands_in_England.svg" class="image" title="West Midlands region in England"><img alt="West Midlands, highlighted in red on a beige political map of England" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/West_Midlands_in_England.svg/200px-West_Midlands_in_England.svg.png" width="200" height="243" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/West_Midlands_in_England.svg/300px-West_Midlands_in_England.svg.png 1.5x, in England</div></td></tr><tr class="mergedtoprow"><th scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Sovereign_state" title="Sovereign state">Sovereign state</a></th><td>\n<a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a></td></tr><tr class="mergedrow"><th scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Countries_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Countries of the United Kingdom">Country</a></th><td>\n<a conurbation">Birmingham</a></td></tr><tr class="mergedtoprow"><th colspan="2" style="text-align:center;text-align:left">Government<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></th></tr><tr class="mergedrow"><th scope="row">&#160;\xe2\x80\xa2&#160;<a href="/wiki/Local_authority_leaders%27_board" title="Local authority leaders&#39; board">Leaders\' board</a></th><td>\n<a Midlands (European Parliament constituency)">West Midlands</a></td></tr><tr class="mergedtoprow"><th colspan="2" style="text-align:center;text-align:left">Area<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></th></tr><tr class="mergedrow"><th scope="row">&#160;\xe2\x80\xa2&#160;Total</th><td>\n5,000&#160;sq&#160;mi (13,000&#160;km<sup>2</sup>)</td></tr><tr class="mergedrow"><th scope="row">Area rank</th><td>\n7th</td></tr><tr class="mergedtoprow"><th colspan="2" style="text-align:center;text-align:left">Population <span style="font-weight:normal">(2014 est.)</span></th></tr><tr class="mergedrow"><th scope="row">&#160;\xe2\x80\xa2&#160;Total</th><td>\n5,713,000</td></tr><tr class="mergedrow"><th scope="row">&#160;\xe2\x80\xa2&#160;Rank</th><td>\n5th</td></tr><tr class="mergedrow"><th scope="row">&#160;\xe2\x80\xa2&#160;Density</th><td>\n1,100/sq&#160;mi (440/km<sup>2</sup>)</td></tr><tr class="mergedtoprow"><th colspan="2" style="text-align:center;text-align:left"><a href="/wiki/Gross_value_added" title="Gross value added">GVA</a><span style="font-weight:normal"></span></th></tr><tr class="mergedrow"><th scope="row">&#160;\xe2\x80\xa2&#160;Total</th><td>\n\xc2\xa3110&#160;billion</td></tr><tr class="mergedrow"><th scope="row">&#160;\xe2\x80\xa2&#160;Per capita</th><td>\n\xc2\xa317,161 (<a href="/wiki/Countries_of_the_United_Kingdom_by_GVA_per_capita" title="Countries of the United Kingdom by GVA per capita">7th</a>)</td></tr><tr class="mergedtoprow"><th scope="row"><a href="/wiki/NUTS_statistical_regions_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="NUTS statistical regions of the United Kingdom">NUTS code</a></th><td>\nUKG</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>The <b>West Midlands</b> is one of nine official <a href="/wiki/Regions_of_England" title="Regions of England">regions of England</a> at the <a href="/wiki/First_level_NUTS_of_the_European_Union" class="mw-redirect" title="First level NUTS of the European Union">first level</a> of <a href="/wiki/NUTS_statistical_regions_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="NUTS statistical regions of the United Kingdom">NUTS</a> for <a href="/wiki/Eurostat" title="Eurostat">statistical</a> purposes. It covers the western half of the area traditionally known as the <a href="/wiki/Midlands_(England)" class="mw-redirect" title="Midlands (England)">Midlands</a>. It contains <a href="/wiki/Birmingham" title="Birmingham">Birmingham</a> and the larger <a href="/wiki/West_Midlands_conurbation" title="West Midlands conurbation">West Midlands conurbation</a>, which includes the city of <a href="/wiki/Wolverhampton" title="Wolverhampton">Wolverhampton</a> and large towns of <a href="/wiki/Dudley" title="Dudley">Dudley</a>, <a href="/wiki/Solihull" title="Solihull">Solihull</a>, <a href="/wiki/Walsall" title="Walsall">Walsall</a> and <a href="/wiki/West_Bromwich" title="West Bromwich">West Bromwich</a>. The City of <a href="/wiki/Coventry" title="Coventry">Coventry</a> (once known as the UK\'s answer to the USA\'s \'Motor City\' Detroit) is also located within the <a href="/wiki/West_Midlands_county" class="mw-redirect" title="West Midlands county">West Midlands county</a>, but is separated from the <a href="/wiki/Conurbation" title="Conurbation">conurbation</a> to the west by several miles of <a href="/wiki/Green_belt" title="Green belt">green belt</a>. The Region also contains 6 shire counties which stretch from the Welsh Border to the East Midlands.\n</p><p>The region is geographically diverse, from the urban central areas of the conurbation to the rural western counties of <a href="/wiki/Shropshire" title="Shropshire">Shropshire</a> and <a href="/wiki/Herefordshire" title="Herefordshire">Herefordshire</a> which border <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a>. The longest river in the UK, the <a href="/wiki/River_Severn" title="River Severn">River Severn</a>, traverses the region southeastwards, flowing through the <a href="/wiki/County_town" title="County town">county towns</a> of <a href="/wiki/Shrewsbury" title="Shrewsbury">Shrewsbury</a> and <a href="/wiki/Worcester" title="Worcester">Worcester</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Ironbridge_Gorge" title="Ironbridge Gorge">Ironbridge Gorge</a>, a <a href="/wiki/UNESCO_World_Heritage_Site" class="mw-redirect" title="UNESCO World Heritage Site">UNESCO World Heritage Site</a>. <a href="/wiki/Staffordshire" title="Staffordshire">Staffordshire</a> is home to the industrialised <a href="/wiki/Staffordshire_Potteries" title="Staffordshire Potteries">Potteries</a> conurbation, including the city of <a href="/wiki/Stoke-on-Trent" title="Stoke-on-Trent">Stoke-on-Trent</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Staffordshire_Moorlands" title="Staffordshire Moorlands">Staffordshire Moorlands</a> area, which borders the southeastern <a href="/wiki/Peak_District_National_Park" class="mw-redirect" title="Peak District National Park">Peak District National Park</a> near <a href="/wiki/Leek,_Staffordshire" title="Leek, Staffordshire">Leek</a>. The region also encompasses five <a href="/wiki/Areas_of_Outstanding_Natural_Beauty" class="mw-redirect" title="Areas of Outstanding Natural Beauty">Areas of Outstanding Natural Beauty</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Wye_Valley" title="Wye Valley">Wye Valley</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shropshire_Hills" title="Shropshire Hills">Shropshire Hills</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cannock_Chase" title="Cannock Chase">Cannock Chase</a>, <a href="/wiki/Malvern_Hills" title="Malvern Hills">Malvern Hills</a>, and parts of the <a href="/wiki/Cotswolds" title="Cotswolds">Cotswolds</a>. <a href="/wiki/Warwickshire" title="Warwickshire">Warwickshire</a> is home to the towns of <a href="/wiki/Stratford_upon_Avon" class="mw-redirect" title="Stratford upon Avon">Stratford upon Avon</a>, birthplace of writer <a href="/wiki/William_Shakespeare" title="William Shakespeare">William Shakespeare</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rugby,_Warwickshire" title="Rugby, Warwickshire">Rugby</a>, the birthplace of <a href="/wiki/Rugby_football" title="Rugby football">Rugby football</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nuneaton" title="Nuneaton">Nuneaton</a> , birthplace to author <a href="/wiki/George_Eliot" title="George Eliot">George Eliot</a>\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" class="tocnumber">10.2.3</span> <span class="toctext">Community Radio</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-39"><a href="#Newspapers"><span class="tocnumber">10.3</span> <span class="toctext">Newspapers</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-40"><a href="#Magazines"><span class="tocnumber">10.4</span> <span class="toctext">Magazines</span></a></li>\n<li class="toctext">Football</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-44"><a href="#Rugby"><span class="tocnumber">11.2</span> <span class="toctext">Rugby</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-48"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">13</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-49"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">14</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=West_Midlands_(region)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Geography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<table class="vertical-navbox vcard" style="float:right;clear:right;width:22.0em;margin:0 0 1.0em 1.0em;background:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;padding:0.2em;border-spacing:0.4em 0;text-align:center;line-height:1.4em;font-size:88%;width:21.0em;"><tbody><tr><th style="padding:0.2em 0.4em England">list</a>)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Unparished_area" title="Unparished area">Unparished Area</a></li></ul></li></ul></div></div></td>\n</tr><tr><td style="padding:0.3em 0.4em 0.3em;font-weight:bold;border-top: 1px solid #aaa; border-bottom: 1px solid #aaa;">\n<a href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_England.svg" class="image"><img alt="Flag of England.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/be/Flag_of_England.svg/32px-Flag_of_England.svg.png" width="32" height="19" class="noviewer" //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/be/Flag_of_England.svg/64px-Flag_of_England.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="480" /></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:England" title="Portal:England">England&#32;portal</a>  <div class="hlist" style="text-align:center;font-weight:normal;">\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Outline_of_political_science#Politics_by_region" title="Outline of political science">Other countries</a></li>\n<li><a href="https://commons.wikimedia.org/wiki/Atlas_of_England" class="extiw" title="commons:Atlas of England">Atlas</a></li></ul>\n</div></td></tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Politics_of_England" title="Template:Politics of England"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Politics_of_England" title="Template talk:Politics of England"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Politics_of_England&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>The official region contains the <a href="/wiki/Ceremonial_counties_of_England" title="Ceremonial counties of England">ceremonial counties</a> of <a href="/wiki/Herefordshire" title="Herefordshire">Herefordshire</a>, Shropshire, <a href="/wiki/Staffordshire" title="Staffordshire">Staffordshire</a>, Warwickshire, <a href="/wiki/West_Midlands_(county)" title="West Midlands (county)">West Midlands</a> and <a href="/wiki/Worcestershire" title="Worcestershire">Worcestershire</a>.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Lyth_Hill_01.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Lyth_Hill_01.jpg/220px-Lyth_Hill_01.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Lyth_Hill_01.jpg/330px-Lyth_Hill_01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Lyth_Hill_01.jpg/440px-Lyth_Hill_01.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="1536" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Lyth_Hill_01.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Rural <a href="/wiki/Shropshire" title="Shropshire">Shropshire</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bayston_Hill" title="Bayston Hill">Lyth Hill</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>There is some confusion in the use of the term "West Midlands", as the name is also used for the much smaller <a href="/wiki/West_Midlands_(county)" title="West Midlands (county)">West Midlands county</a> and conurbation which is in the central belt of the Midlands and on the eastern side of the West Midlands Region. It is also still used by various organisations within that area, such as <a href="/wiki/West_Midlands_Police" title="West Midlands Police">West Midlands Police</a> and <a href="/wiki/West_Midlands_Fire_Service" title="West Midlands Fire Service">West Midlands Fire Service</a>.\n</p><p>The highest point in the region is <a href="/wiki/Black_Mountain_(hill)" title="Black Mountain (hill)">Black Mountain</a>, at 703&#160;metres (2,307&#160;ft) in west Herefordshire on the border with <a href="/wiki/Powys" title="Powys">Powys</a>, Wales.\n</p><p>The region contains five <a href="/wiki/Areas_of_Outstanding_Natural_Beauty" class="mw-redirect" title="Areas of Outstanding Natural Beauty">Areas of Outstanding Natural Beauty</a> (AONBs), including the <a href="/wiki/Shropshire_Hills" title="Shropshire Hills">Shropshire Hills</a>, <a href="/wiki/Malvern_Hills" title="Malvern Hills">Malvern Hills</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cannock_Chase" title="Cannock Chase">Cannock Chase</a>, and parts of the <a href="/wiki/Wye_Valley" title="Wye Valley">Wye Valley</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cotswolds" title="Cotswolds">Cotswolds</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Peak_District" title="Peak District">Peak District</a> national park also stretches into the northern corner of <a href="/wiki/Staffordshire" title="Staffordshire">Staffordshire</a>.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Transport">Transport</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=West_Midlands_(region)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Transport">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Railways">Railways</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=West_Midlands_(region)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Railways">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Served by many lines in the urban areas such as the <a href="/wiki/West_Coast_Main_Line" title="West Coast Main Line">West Coast Main Line</a> and branches.  The <a href="/wiki/Welsh_Marches_Line" class="mw-redirect" title="Welsh Marches Line">Welsh Marches Line</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Cotswold_Line" title="Cotswold Line">Cotswold Line</a> transect the region as well as the <a href="/wiki/Cross_Country_Route" title="Cross Country Route">Cross Country Route</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chiltern_Line" class="mw-redirect" title="Chiltern Line">Chiltern Line</a>.  There are plans to reopen the <a href="/wiki/Honeybourne_Line" class="mw-redirect" title="Honeybourne Line">Honeybourne Line</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Road">Road</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=West_Midlands_(region)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Road">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:M6_Toll_plaza,_Great_Wyrley.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/M6_Toll_plaza%2C_Great_Wyrley.jpg/220px-M6_Toll_plaza%2C_Great_Wyrley.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/M6_Toll_plaza%2C_Great_Wyrley.jpg/330px-M6_Toll_plaza%2C_Great_Wyrley.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/M6_Toll_plaza%2C_Great_Wyrley.jpg/440px-M6_Toll_plaza%2C_Great_Wyrley.jpg 2x" data-file-width="610" data-file-height="407" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:M6_Toll_plaza,_Great_Wyrley.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/M6_Toll" title="M6 Toll">M6 Toll</a> plaza.</div></div></div>\n<p>Numerous notable roads pass through the region, with most converging around the central conurbation. The <a href="/wiki/M5_motorway" title="M5 motorway">M5</a>, which connects <a href="/wiki/South_West_England" title="South West England">South West England</a> to the region, passes through Worcestershire, near to <a href="/wiki/Worcester" title="Worcester">Worcester</a>, and through the <a href="/wiki/West_Midlands_county" class="mw-redirect" title="West Midlands county">West Midlands county</a>, past <a href="/wiki/West_Bromwich" title="West Bromwich">West Bromwich</a>, with its northern terminus at its junction with the <a href="/wiki/M6_motorway" title="M6 motorway">M6</a> just south of <a href="/wiki/Walsall" title="Walsall">Walsall</a>. The M6, which has its southern terminus just outside the southeast of the region at its junction with the <a href="/wiki/M1_motorway" title="M1 motorway">M1</a>, and which connects the region to <a href="/wiki/North_West_England" title="North West England">North West England</a>, passes <a href="/wiki/Rugby,_Warwickshire" title="Rugby, Warwickshire">Rugby</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nuneaton" title="Nuneaton">Nuneaton</a> in Warwickshire, <a href="/wiki/Coventry" title="Coventry">Coventry</a> and <a href="/wiki/Birmingham" title="Birmingham">Birmingham</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Stafford" title="Stafford">Stafford</a> and <a href="/wiki/Stoke-on-Trent" title="Stoke-on-Trent">Stoke-on-Trent</a> in Staffordshire. The <a href="/wiki/M6_toll" class="mw-redirect" title="M6 toll">M6 toll</a> provides an alternative route to the M6 between <a href="/wiki/Coleshill,_Warwickshire" title="Coleshill, Warwickshire">Coleshill</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cannock" title="Cannock">Cannock</a>, passing north of <a href="/wiki/Sutton_Coldfield" title="Sutton Coldfield">Sutton Coldfield</a> and just south of <a href="/wiki/Lichfield" title="Lichfield">Lichfield</a>.  The <a href="/wiki/M40_motorway" title="M40 motorway">M40</a> connects the region through <a href="/wiki/South_East_England" title="South East England">South East England</a> to London, with its northern terminus at its junction with the <a href="/wiki/M42_motorway" title="M42 motorway">M42</a>; it passes close to <a href="/wiki/Warwick" title="Warwick">Warwick</a> and <a href="/wiki/Banbury" title="Banbury">Banbury</a>. The M42 connects the M5 at <a href="/wiki/Bromsgrove" title="Bromsgrove">Bromsgrove</a>, passing around the south and east of Birmingham, joining the M40 and M6, passing <a href="/wiki/Solihull" title="Solihull">Solihull</a> and <a href="/wiki/Castle_Bromwich" title="Castle Bromwich">Castle Bromwich</a>, to <a href="/wiki/Tamworth,_Staffordshire" title="Tamworth, Staffordshire">Tamworth</a>, northeast of Birmingham.  The <a href="/wiki/M50_motorway_(Great_Britain)" title="M50 motorway (Great Britain)">M50</a> connects the M5 from near <a href="/wiki/Tewkesbury" title="Tewkesbury">Tewkesbury</a> to <a href="/wiki/Ross-on-Wye" title="Ross-on-Wye">Ross-on-Wye</a> in the southwest. The <a href="/wiki/M54_motorway" title="M54 motorway">M54</a> connects <a href="/wiki/Wellington,_Shropshire" title="Wellington, Shropshire">Wellington</a> in the west, passing <a href="/wiki/Telford" title="Telford">Telford</a>, to the M6 near <a href="/wiki/Cannock" title="Cannock">Cannock</a>. The <a href="/wiki/A5_road_(Great_Britain)" title="A5 road (Great Britain)">A5 road</a> traverses the region northwest-southeast, passing through <a href="/wiki/Shrewsbury" title="Shrewsbury">Shrewsbury</a>, <a href="/wiki/Telford" title="Telford">Telford</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cannock" title="Cannock">Cannock</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tamworth,_Staffordshire" title="Tamworth, Staffordshire">Tamworth</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nuneaton" title="Nuneaton">Nuneaton</a>.\n</p><p>The longest elevated road viaduct in the UK is the 3 miles (4,779&#160;m) section from Gravelly Hill to Castle Bromwich on the M6, opened on 24 May 1972; the 3.5 miles (5.6&#160;km) <a href="/wiki/Bromford_Viaduct" title="Bromford Viaduct">Bromford Viaduct</a> is the longest viaduct in the UK. The section of the A45 in Coventry from <a href="/wiki/Willenhall,_Coventry" title="Willenhall, Coventry">Willenhall</a> to Allesley in 1939 was one of the UK\'s first ever large planned road schemes; road schemes on that scale had not been previously built, with few large road schemes outside of London, or were piecemeal. \n</p><p>Princes Square in Wolverhampton had Britain\'s first automatic traffic lights on 5 November 1927.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> On 13 January 2012, 34-year-old Ben Westwood of Wednesfield, was caught by the police, when speeding at 180mph, in an <a href="/wiki/Audi_A5" title="Audi A5">Audi RS5</a> with a <a href="/wiki/Lamborghini" title="Lamborghini">Lamborghini</a> engine, from Wolverhampton up to Stafford on the M6, and back again. He was travelling so fast that he was outpacing the <a href="/wiki/Central_Counties_Air_Operations_Unit" title="Central Counties Air Operations Unit">Central Counties Air Operations Unit</a> <a href="/wiki/Eurocopter_EC135" title="Eurocopter EC135">Eurocopter</a> helicopter. He and the vehicle had been in fifteen <a href="/wiki/Smash_and_grab" title="Smash and grab">smash and grab</a> raids and he was jailed for nine years at Wolverhampton Crown Court in August 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Transport_policy">Transport policy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=West_Midlands_(region)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Transport policy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:M45_Motorway_near_Barby_-_geograph.org.uk_-_31743.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/M45_Motorway_near_Barby_-_geograph.org.uk_-_31743.jpg/220px-M45_Motorway_near_Barby_-_geograph.org.uk_-_31743.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/M45_Motorway_near_Barby_-_geograph.org.uk_-_31743.jpg/330px-M45_Motorway_near_Barby_-_geograph.org.uk_-_31743.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/M45_Motorway_near_Barby_-_geograph.org.uk_-_31743.jpg/440px-M45_Motorway_near_Barby_-_geograph.org.uk_-_31743.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="480" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:M45_Motorway_near_Barby_-_geograph.org.uk_-_31743.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/M45_motorway" title="M45 motorway">M45 motorway</a>, originally called the <a href="/wiki/Dunchurch" title="Dunchurch">Dunchurch</a> Link, was one of the first motorways built in the UK, and is now one of the quietest</div></div></div>\n<p>As part of the transport planning system, the Regional Assembly is under statutory requirement to produce a <a href="/w/index.php?title=Regional_Transport_Strategy_(UK)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Regional Transport Strategy (UK) (page does not exist)">Regional Transport Strategy (RTS)</a> to provide long term planning for transport in the region. This involves region wide transport schemes such as those carried out by the <a href="/wiki/Highways_Agency" class="mw-redirect" title="Highways Agency">Highways Agency</a> and <a href="/wiki/Network_Rail" title="Network Rail">Network Rail</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-GoWM1_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GoWM1-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Within the region, the local transport authorities carry out transport planning through the use of a <a href="/wiki/Local_transport_plan" title="Local transport plan">Local transport plan</a> (LTP) which outlines their strategies, policies and implementation programme.<sup id="cite_ref-DfT1_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DfT1-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> The most recent LTP is that for the period 2006\xe2\x80\x9311. In the West Midlands region, the following transport authorities have published their LTP online: <a href="/wiki/Herefordshire" title="Herefordshire">Herefordshire</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-HLTP_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HLTP-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Shropshire" title="Shropshire">Shropshire</a> U.A.,<sup id="cite_ref-ShLTP_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ShLTP-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Staffordshire" title="Staffordshire">Staffordshire</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-StLTP_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-StLTP-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Telford_and_Wrekin" title="Telford and Wrekin">Telford and Wrekin</a> U.A.,<sup id="cite_ref-T&amp;WLTP_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-T&amp;WLTP-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Warwickshire" title="Warwickshire">Warwickshire</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-WrLTP_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WrLTP-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/West_Midlands_(county)" title="West Midlands (county)">West Midlands</a><sup id="cite_ref-WMLTP_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WMLTP-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Worcestershire" title="Worcestershire">Worcestershire</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-WcLTP_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WcLTP-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> The transport authority of <a href="/wiki/Stoke-on-Trent" title="Stoke-on-Trent">Stoke-on-Trent</a> U.A. publishes a Joint Local Transport Plan in partnership with Staffordshire County Council to cover the North Staffordshire Major Urban Area, which includes Stoke-on-Trent and the more urban parts of <a href="/wiki/Newcastle-under-Lyme" title="Newcastle-under-Lyme">Newcastle-under-Lyme</a> and <a href="/wiki/Staffordshire_Moorlands" title="Staffordshire Moorlands">Staffordshire Moorlands</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-SoTLTP_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SoTLTP-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Towns_and_cities">Towns and cities</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span Green</span>.</div></div></div>\n<p>&#32;\n</p>\n</td></tr></tbody></table></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_history">Modern history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=West_Midlands_(region)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Modern history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Mercia" title="Mercia">Mercia</a></div>\n<p>The region is largely based on the heartland of the former Anglo-Saxon kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Mercia" title="Mercia">Mercia</a>, which had its capital in Tamworth. After it became part of England, the region was still referred to as Mercia until it eventually began being named the Midlands around 1555, with this specific area called the West Midlands. \n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Edgehill" title="Battle of Edgehill">Battle of Edgehill</a> in October 1642 started the <a href="/wiki/English_Civil_War" title="English Civil War">English Civil War</a>; the site is now found between the B4086 and A422 in south Warwickshire, in the parish of <a href="/wiki/Kineton" title="Kineton">Kineton</a>.\n</p><p><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nicky_Smith_(aviator)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Nicky Smith (aviator) (page does not exist)">Nicky Smith</a> was the RAF\'s first female helicopter pilot, qualifying on 16 October 1992; she had joined the 1989 Course on <a href="/wiki/No._2_Flying_Training_School_RAF" title="No. 2 Flying Training School RAF">2 FTS</a>; on 20 July 1989 it had been announced that female RAF pilots could fly in non-combat roles. <a href="/wiki/Jane_Bunford" title="Jane Bunford">Jane Bunford</a> of <a href="/wiki/Bartley_Green" title="Bartley Green">Bartley Green</a> until 1982 was the tallest woman ever in the world, and now the second tallest, and the tallest person ever in the UK, at 7&#160;ft 11in.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Heart_of_England_Forest" title="Heart of England Forest">Heart of England Forest</a> is the largest privately created forest in the world.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="World_War_II">World War II</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=West_Midlands_(region)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: World War II">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/RAF_Fauld_explosion" title="RAF Fauld explosion">RAF Fauld explosion</a> on 27 November 1944 in east Staffordshire produced a 100-foot deep crater, and is the UK\'s largest explosion, being caused by around 4,000 tonnes of <a href="/wiki/Explosive_material" class="mw-redirect" title="Explosive material">high explosive</a>, and may be the <a href="/wiki/Largest_artificial_non-nuclear_explosions" title="Largest artificial non-nuclear explosions">world\'s largest non-nuclear explosion</a>.\n</p><p>Birmingham was the third <a href="/wiki/Birmingham_Blitz" title="Birmingham Blitz">most bombed</a> city in the UK after London and <a href="/wiki/Liverpool" title="Liverpool">Liverpool</a>; Spitfires were built in Castle Bromwich, <a href="/wiki/Avro_Lancaster" title="Avro Lancaster">Lancasters</a> at Austin\'s works in <a href="/wiki/Longbridge_plant" title="Longbridge plant">Longbridge</a> at <a href="/wiki/Cofton_Hackett" title="Cofton Hackett">Cofton Hackett</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Birmingham_Small_Arms_Company" title="Birmingham Small Arms Company">Birmingham Small Arms Company</a> at <a href="/wiki/Small_Heath,_Birmingham" class="mw-redirect" title="Small Heath, Birmingham">Small Heath</a> produced the <a href="/wiki/M1919_Browning_machine_gun" title="M1919 Browning machine gun">M1919 Browning machine gun</a>. <a href="/wiki/Boulton_Paul_Aircraft" title="Boulton Paul Aircraft">Boulton Paul Aircraft</a> had their main aircraft factory in the north of Wolverhampton. <a href="/wiki/RAF_Defford" title="RAF Defford">RAF Defford</a>, in the south of Worcestershire between Pershore and <a href="/wiki/Croome_Court" title="Croome Court">Croome Park</a>, was where many important <a href="/wiki/Airborne_early_warning_and_control" title="Airborne early warning and control">airborne radars</a> were developed, such as <a href="/wiki/H2S_(radar)" title="H2S (radar)">H2S (radar)</a> and anti-submarine radars.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Scientific_heritage">Scientific heritage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=West_Midlands_(region)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Scientific heritage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Thomas_Wedgwood_(photographer)" title="Thomas Wedgwood (photographer)">Thomas Wedgwood</a>, son of Josiah Wedgwood, discovered the first <a href="/wiki/Photographic_paper" title="Photographic paper">photo-sensitive</a> (light-sensitive) chemicals \xe2\x80\x93 <a href="/wiki/Silver_nitrate" title="Silver nitrate">silver nitrate</a> and <a href="/wiki/Silver_chloride" title="Silver chloride">silver chloride</a> in the 1790s.\n</p><p>Sir <a href="/wiki/Norman_Lockyer" title="Norman Lockyer">Norman Lockyer</a> of <a href="/wiki/Kenilworth" title="Kenilworth">Kenilworth</a> discovered <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a> in 1868, for which he used electromagnetic <a href="/wiki/Spectroscopy" title="Spectroscopy">spectroscopy</a>.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Edward_Weston_(chemist)" title="Edward Weston (chemist)">Edward Weston</a> of Oswestry, who emigrated to the US, built the first accurate <a href="/wiki/Voltmeter" title="Voltmeter">voltmeter</a> in the late 1880s, and the <a href="/wiki/Weston_cell" title="Weston cell">Weston cell</a> in 1893. \n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Francis_William_Aston" title="Francis William Aston">Francis W. Aston</a> of Harborne, educated at the University of Birmingham, developed <a href="/wiki/Mass_spectrometry" title="Mass spectrometry">mass spectrometry</a> in 1919, which helped him to identify the first <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">isotopes</a>, receiving the <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Chemistry" title="Nobel Prize in Chemistry">Nobel Prize for Chemistry</a> in 1922.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Dennis_Gabor" title="Dennis Gabor">Dennis Gabor</a> invented holography at <a href="/wiki/British_Thomson-Houston" title="British Thomson-Houston">British Thomson-Houston</a> in Rugby in 1947, receiving the <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Physics" title="Nobel Prize in Physics">Nobel Prize for Physics</a> in 1971. \n</p><p><a href="/wiki/James_Glaisher" title="James Glaisher">James Glaisher</a> in 1862 took a record balloon flight with <a href="/wiki/Henry_Tracey_Coxwell" title="Henry Tracey Coxwell">Henry Tracey Coxwell</a> for the <a href="/wiki/British_Science_Association" title="British Science Association">BAAS</a> near Wolverhampton. They reached 29,000 feet (8,800&#160;m) the composition of the Earth\'s atmosphere until then was not understood; the altitude records for the UK have not been exceeded since; <a href="/wiki/Project_Excelsior" title="Project Excelsior">Project Excelsior</a> in the USA in 1960 would later reach 20 miles (110,000&#160;ft).\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Philip_Lawley" title="Philip Lawley">Philip Lawley</a> of Burton upon Trent was first person to realise that <a href="/wiki/Health_effects_of_tobacco" title="Health effects of tobacco">chemical damage to DNA</a> caused cancer (at the Chester Beatty Research Institute in London) in the early 1960s.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Francis_Galton" title="Francis Galton">Francis Galton</a> (d. 1911) of the <a href="/wiki/Darwin%E2%80%93Wedgwood_family" title="Darwin\xe2\x80\x93Wedgwood family">Darwin\xe2\x80\x93Wedgwood family</a>\'s Birmingham branch was an early <a href="/wiki/Eugenics" title="Eugenics">eugenicist</a> rooted in improving animal breeding stock and examining heredity. He invented terms eugenics and <i><a href="/wiki/Nature_versus_nurture" title="Nature versus nurture">nature versus nurture</a></i>. His limited calls for human eugenics were widened by the <a href="/wiki/German_Society_for_Racial_Hygiene" title="German Society for Racial Hygiene">German Society for Racial Hygiene</a> in 1905 founded by <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Ploetz" title="Alfred Ploetz">Alfred Ploetz</a>, which coupled with the racial superiority fallacies of <a href="/wiki/Aryan_race" title="Aryan race">Aryanism</a> reached its nadir in <a href="/wiki/Holocaust" class="mw-redirect" title="Holocaust">genocidal</a> <a href="/wiki/Anti-semitism" class="mw-redirect" title="Anti-semitism">anti-semitism</a>. Moral teachings and <a href="/wiki/Deontological_ethics" title="Deontological ethics">inherent replusions</a> towards human eugenics were overcome by a minority of those in power espousing <a href="/wiki/Racial_equality" title="Racial equality">racial equality</a>; European media and leaders lamented <a href="/wiki/European_Imperialism" class="mw-redirect" title="European Imperialism">loss of Empire</a>, advocated <a href="/wiki/Ultranationalism" title="Ultranationalism">ultranationalism</a> and prized military physical advantage; Galton saw human eugenics as part of all means to <i>do better</i>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Industrial_heritage">Industrial heritage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=West_Midlands_(region)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Industrial heritage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Science_and_invention_in_Birmingham" title="Science and invention in Birmingham">Science and invention in Birmingham</a> and <a href="/wiki/Architecture_of_Birmingham" title="Architecture of Birmingham">Architecture of Birmingham</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Iron_Bridge_-_geograph.org.uk_-_224230.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/The_Iron_Bridge_-_geograph.org.uk_-_224230.jpg/220px-The_Iron_Bridge_-_geograph.org.uk_-_224230.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/The_Iron_Bridge_-_geograph.org.uk_-_224230.jpg/330px-The_Iron_Bridge_-_geograph.org.uk_-_224230.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/The_Iron_Bridge_-_geograph.org.uk_-_224230.jpg/440px-The_Iron_Bridge_-_geograph.org.uk_-_224230.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="427" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Iron_Bridge_-_geograph.org.uk_-_224230.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Cast_iron" title="Cast iron">cast iron</a> Iron Bridge at <a href="/wiki/Coalbrookdale" title="Coalbrookdale">Coalbrookdale</a>, opened in January 1781 and was the first large-scale object made out of cast iron; but cast iron is not reliably strong due to impurities. <a href="/wiki/Wrought_iron" title="Wrought iron">Wrought iron</a>, where the carbon is hammered to remove to carbon and impurities is much stronger; the first large-scale wrought iron bridge was the <a href="/wiki/Britannia_Bridge" title="Britannia Bridge">Britannia Bridge</a> over the <a href="/wiki/Menai_Strait" title="Menai Strait">Menai Strait</a>, only possible due to its innovative <a href="/wiki/Box_girder" title="Box girder">box girder</a> design by <a href="/wiki/Robert_Stephenson" title="Robert Stephenson">Robert Stephenson</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Much of the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_revolution" class="mw-redirect" title="Industrial revolution">Industrial revolution</a> in the United Kingdom began in <a href="/wiki/Birmingham" title="Birmingham">Birmingham</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Black_Country" title="Black Country">Black Country</a> area of West Midlands. The Industrial Revolution is thought to have begun when <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Darby_I" title="Abraham Darby I">Abraham Darby</a> substituted <a href="/wiki/Coke_(fuel)" title="Coke (fuel)">coke</a> in the place of charcoal to smelt iron, at his Old Furnace. The Black Country may be regarded as the world\'s first industrial landscape,<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> while nearby <a href="/wiki/Ironbridge_Gorge" title="Ironbridge Gorge">Ironbridge Gorge</a> claims to be the Birthplace of Industry.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/The_Iron_Bridge" title="The Iron Bridge">The world\'s first cast iron bridge in 1779</a> spans the Gorge. The first self-propelled locomotive to run on rails in 1803 at Coalbrookdale, was built by <a href="/wiki/Richard_Trevithick" title="Richard Trevithick">Richard Trevithick</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Permanent_way_(history)" title="Permanent way (history)">first iron rails</a> for horse-drawn transport, were made at Coalbrookdale in 1768 by <a href="/wiki/Richard_Reynolds_(ironmaster)" title="Richard Reynolds (ironmaster)">Richard Reynolds</a> at <a href="/wiki/Ketley_Ironworks" title="Ketley Ironworks">Ketley Ironworks</a>. Iron rails only became widely successful in 1820 when made out of <a href="/wiki/Wrought_iron" title="Wrought iron">wrought iron</a> at <a href="/wiki/Bedlington_Ironworks" title="Bedlington Ironworks">Bedlington Ironworks</a> in north-east England.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Philipp_Jakob_Loutherbourg_d._J._002.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Philipp_Jakob_Loutherbourg_d._J._002.jpg/220px-Philipp_Jakob_Loutherbourg_d._J._002.jpg" width="220" height="141" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Philipp_Jakob_Loutherbourg_d._J._002.jpg/330px-Philipp_Jakob_Loutherbourg_d._J._002.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Philipp_Jakob_Loutherbourg_d._J._002.jpg/440px-Philipp_Jakob_Loutherbourg_d._J._002.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1571" data-file-height="1009" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Philipp_Jakob_Loutherbourg_d._J._002.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Coalbrookdale by Night</i>, of the <a href="/wiki/Madeley_Wood_Company" title="Madeley Wood Company">Madeley Wood Company</a>, painted by <a href="/wiki/Philip_James_de_Loutherbourg" title="Philip James de Loutherbourg">Philip James de Loutherbourg</a> in 1801</div></div></div> \n<p>Birmingham\'s industrial development was triggered by discussions at the <a href="/wiki/Lunar_Society_of_Birmingham" title="Lunar Society of Birmingham">Lunar Society of Birmingham</a> at <a href="/wiki/Soho_House" title="Soho House">Soho House</a>, Boulton\'s house, and products were carried along the <a href="/wiki/BCN_Main_Line" title="BCN Main Line">BCN Main Line</a> canal. <a href="/wiki/Soho_Manufactory" title="Soho Manufactory">Soho Manufactory</a> was the first man-made-powered factory in world. <a href="/wiki/Chance_Brothers" title="Chance Brothers">Chance Brothers</a> of Smethwick built the glass for <a href="/wiki/The_Crystal_Palace" title="The Crystal Palace">The Crystal Palace</a> in 1851. <a href="/wiki/Smethwick_Engine" title="Smethwick Engine">Smethwick Engine</a>, now at <a href="/wiki/Thinktank,_Birmingham_Science_Museum" title="Thinktank, Birmingham Science Museum">Thinktank, Birmingham Science Museum</a>, is the oldest working steam engine, made in 1779, and is the oldest working engine in the world. Smethwick was a main centre for making <a href="/wiki/Lighthouse" title="Lighthouse">lighthouse</a> <a href="/wiki/Lantern" title="Lantern">lanterns</a>.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Yale_%26_Valor" title="Yale &amp; Valor">Valor Fires</a> in Erdington developed the first <a href="/wiki/Radiant_heating" title="Radiant heating">radiant</a> <a href="/wiki/Gas_heater" title="Gas heater">gas fire</a> in 1967, a balanced <a href="/wiki/Flue" title="Flue">flue</a> fire in 1973, and a natural flame gas fire in 1978. The Erdington site, owned by Iceland\'s <a href="/wiki/BDR_Thermea" title="BDR Thermea">BDR Thermea</a>, closed in May 2012. The company also built <a href="/wiki/Gas_stove" title="Gas stove">gas cookers</a>; since 2011 the company has been part of <a href="/wiki/Glen_Dimplex" class="mw-redirect" title="Glen Dimplex">Glen Dimplex</a>, who have a site at <a href="/w/index.php?title=Cooper%27s_Bank&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Cooper&#39;s Bank (page does not exist)">Cooper\'s Bank</a>, south of <a href="/wiki/Gornal,_West_Midlands" title="Gornal, West Midlands">Gornalwood</a>.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Boulton,_Watt_and_Murdoch_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1754695.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Boulton%2C_Watt_and_Murdoch_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1754695.jpg/220px-Boulton%2C_Watt_and_Murdoch_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1754695.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Boulton%2C_Watt_and_Murdoch_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1754695.jpg/330px-Boulton%2C_Watt_and_Murdoch_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1754695.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Boulton%2C_Watt_and_Murdoch_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1754695.jpg/440px-Boulton%2C_Watt_and_Murdoch_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1754695.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="480" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Boulton,_Watt_and_Murdoch_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1754695.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Boulton, Watt and Murdoch, a 1956 statue on Broad Street in Birmingham; the <a href="/wiki/International_System_of_Units" title="International System of Units">SI</a> unit of power is the watt, most commonly found as the kW, a replacement for the imperial measurement of <a href="/wiki/Horsepower" title="Horsepower">horsepower</a></div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Ditherington_Flax_Mill" title="Ditherington Flax Mill">Ditherington Flax Mill</a> in Shrewsbury was the first <a href="/wiki/Steel_frame" title="Steel frame">iron-framed building</a> in the world in 1797. <a href="/w/index.php?title=Thomas_Bolton_%26_Sons&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Thomas Bolton &amp; Sons (page does not exist)">Thomas Bolton &amp; Sons</a> of <a href="/wiki/Froghall" title="Froghall">Froghall</a>, Staffordshire, made the world\'s first <a href="/wiki/Transatlantic_telegraph_cable" title="Transatlantic telegraph cable">transatlantic telegraph cable</a> in 1857, having supplied a <a href="/wiki/Submarine_communications_cable" title="Submarine communications cable">submarine cable</a> across the English Channel in 1850. <a href="/w/index.php?title=GEC_Telecommunications&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="GEC Telecommunications (page does not exist)">GEC Telecommunications</a> was headquartered at the <a href="/w/index.php?title=GEC_Telephone_and_Radio_Works&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="GEC Telephone and Radio Works (page does not exist)">GEC Telephone and Radio Works</a> in Coventry, it has now become the New Century Park, off the <a href="/wiki/A428_road" title="A428 road">A428</a> north of <a href="/wiki/Stoke_Aldermoor" title="Stoke Aldermoor">Stoke Aldermoor</a> in eastern Coventry. On 10 July 1890, a trunk circuit telephone line was opened between London and Birmingham by the <a href="/wiki/National_Telephone_Company" title="National Telephone Company">National Telephone Company</a>; for the first time this allowed phone calls between the London and the north.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> The world\'s first <a href="/wiki/Coaxial_cable" title="Coaxial cable">coaxial cable</a> was laid between London and Birmingham in 1936 to give 40 channels for telephone traffic.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> and brought into use in 1938, later extended to Manchester in 1940.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:BaskervilleSpec.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/BaskervilleSpec.svg/220px-BaskervilleSpec.svg.png" width="220" height="264" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/BaskervilleSpec.svg/330px-BaskervilleSpec.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/BaskervilleSpec.svg/440px-BaskervilleSpec.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="200" data-file-height="240" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:BaskervilleSpec.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Baskerville" title="Baskerville">Baskerville</a> font named after the inventor of typefaces</div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Alexander_Parkes" title="Alexander Parkes">Alexander Parkes</a> invented the first man-made plastic (<a href="/wiki/Thermoplastic" title="Thermoplastic">thermoplastic</a>) in Birmingham in 1856. <a href="/w/index.php?title=Arthur_Leslie_Large&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Arthur Leslie Large (page does not exist)">Arthur Leslie Large</a> of Birmingham is credited with inventing the <a href="/wiki/Kettle" title="Kettle">kettle</a> in 1922. Princess Square, Wolverhampton, was the site of Britain\'s first traffic lights in 1927. Infra-red cameras were developed at the Royal Radar Establishment in Malvern (with EMI Electronics) in 1967. The world\'s first <a href="/wiki/Maglev" title="Maglev">Maglev</a> train operated Birmingham Airport in 1983. The tallest freestanding structure in the region is the chimney of <a href="/wiki/Ironbridge_power_stations" title="Ironbridge power stations">Ironbridge power station</a> at 673&#160;ft. <a href="/wiki/John_Baskerville" title="John Baskerville">John Baskerville</a> of Birmingham, a former stone carver, largely invented fonts, or <a href="/wiki/Typeface" title="Typeface">typefaces</a>, for printing. \n</p>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Automotive_industry_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Automotive industry in the United Kingdom">Automotive industry in the United Kingdom</a></div>\n<p>Much of the UK\'s car industry would be centred in Coventry and Birmingham; most of this has now gone. <a href="/w/index.php?title=Midland_Motor_Cylinder&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Midland Motor Cylinder (page does not exist)">Midland Motor Cylinder</a> (part of <a href="/w/index.php?title=Birmid_Industries&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Birmid Industries (page does not exist)">Birmid Industries</a>) of Smethwick was the largest producer of automobile <a href="/wiki/Cylinder_block" title="Cylinder block">cylinder blocks</a> in Europe. <a href="/wiki/Fort_Dunlop" title="Fort Dunlop">Fort Dunlop</a> was Europe\'s largest tyre plant. <a href="/wiki/Metro_Cammell" title="Metro Cammell">Metro Cammell</a> in Birmingham made most of the 1970s and 1980s <a href="/wiki/London_Underground" title="London Underground">LU</a> underground trains. The MG Rover (a company of Rover) closed in 2005 (from 1885), The <a href="/wiki/Ryton_plant" title="Ryton plant">Ryton plant</a>, which made the <a href="/wiki/Peugeot_206" title="Peugeot 206">206</a>, closed at the end of 2006, with production moving to <a href="/wiki/Trnava" title="Trnava">Trnava</a> in Slovakia, and some to a plant at <a href="/wiki/Kol%C3%ADn" title="Kol\xc3\xadn">Kol\xc3\xadn</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Czech_Republic" title="Czech Republic">Czech Republic</a>. <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Herbert_(company)" title="Alfred Herbert (company)">Alfred Herbert</a> of Coventry was the largest <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tool_builder&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Tool builder (page does not exist)">machine-tool manufacturer</a> in the UK for many decades; it was brought down in the 1970s by advancing technology overseas, and complacent strategic decisions of the management (caught like a rabbit in the headlights), finally closing in 1982; many Midlands manufacturing companies followed similar fates in the 1970s and 1980s. Tube Investments was based at <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tube_Investments_House&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Tube Investments House (page does not exist)">Tube Investments House</a>, <a href="/wiki/Five_Ways,_Birmingham" title="Five Ways, Birmingham">Five Ways</a>, Edgbaston (now a <a href="/wiki/Marriott_International" title="Marriott International">Marriott</a> hotel).\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Grazebrook_Beam_Engine.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Grazebrook_Beam_Engine.jpg/220px-Grazebrook_Beam_Engine.jpg" width="220" height="149" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Grazebrook_Beam_Engine.jpg/330px-Grazebrook_Beam_Engine.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Grazebrook_Beam_Engine.jpg/440px-Grazebrook_Beam_Engine.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="432" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Grazebrook_Beam_Engine.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>1817 Boulton &amp; Watt beam blowing engine re-erected on the Dartmouth Circus roundabout, on the <a href="/wiki/A4540_road" title="A4540 road">A4540</a> and the A38(M)</div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/w/index.php?title=Henry_Wiggin_%26_Co&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Henry Wiggin &amp; Co (page does not exist)">Henry Wiggin &amp; Co</a> of Hereford developed the metal <a href="/wiki/Alloy" title="Alloy">alloys</a> necessary for other Midlands\' (and beyond) automotive and aerospace companies \xe2\x80\x93 <a href="/wiki/Inconel" title="Inconel">Inconel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Incoloy" title="Incoloy">Incoloy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nimonic" title="Nimonic">Nimonic</a>. It was the lack of <a href="/wiki/Vanadium" title="Vanadium">vanadium</a> for <a href="/wiki/High-temperature_corrosion" title="High-temperature corrosion">high-melting point alloys</a>, caused by Royal Navy action, that prevented German <a href="/wiki/Messerschmitt_Me_262" title="Messerschmitt Me 262">Me 262</a> engines being serviceable; had German World War 2 engineers had a greater supply of vanadium and <a href="/wiki/Molybdenum" title="Molybdenum">molybdenum</a>, the engine life (around 12 hours maximum, from entering service in April 1944 to the end of the war) of their <a href="/wiki/Junkers_Jumo_004" title="Junkers Jumo 004">jet engine</a> would have increased much more, which would have been significant to the war\'s outcome. <a href="/wiki/Bristol_Siddeley" title="Bristol Siddeley">Bristol Siddeley</a> developed the rocket engines for <a href="/wiki/Black_Arrow" title="Black Arrow">Black Arrow</a> at Ansty; in fact all of R-R\'s rocket engines were developed and built there at R-R\'s Industrial and Marine Gas Turbine Division; Britain\'s smaller rocket engines for missiles were built by <a href="/wiki/Bristol_Aerojet" title="Bristol Aerojet">Bristol Aerojet</a> in what is now <a href="/wiki/North_Somerset" title="North Somerset">North Somerset</a>. High Duty Alloys at Redditch constructed (forged) the compressor and turbine blades for Whittle\'s first engines, and many of the early jet engines; it made <a href="/wiki/Concorde" title="Concorde">Concorde</a>\'s airframe from the <a href="/wiki/Hiduminium" title="Hiduminium">Hiduminium</a> R.R.58 <a href="/wiki/Aluminium_alloy" title="Aluminium alloy">aluminium alloy</a>.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Maxaret" title="Maxaret">Maxaret</a>, the world\'s first <a href="/wiki/Anti-lock_braking_system" title="Anti-lock braking system">ABS</a> <a href="/wiki/Brake" title="Brake">braking</a> system, was invented in Coventry by Dunlop in the early 1950s for aircraft; <a href="/wiki/John_Boyd_Dunlop" title="John Boyd Dunlop">John Boyd Dunlop</a> was a Scottish vet who had first produced the first pneumatic <a href="/wiki/Tire" title="Tire">tyres</a> in 1889. <a href="/wiki/Matthew_Piers_Watt_Boulton" title="Matthew Piers Watt Boulton">Matthew Piers Watt Boulton</a>, grandson of Matthew Boulton, and born in the area, invented the <a href="/wiki/Aileron" title="Aileron">aileron</a>, an important <a href="/wiki/Flight_control_surfaces" title="Flight control surfaces">flight control surface</a> in 1868, decades before the first actual flight. <a href="/wiki/Triumph_Engineering" title="Triumph Engineering">Triumph Engineering</a> was a famous motorbike firm in Meriden. About a quarter of all British WWI planes were built in Coventry. The <a href="/wiki/Jensen_Interceptor" title="Jensen Interceptor">Jensen Interceptor</a> FF was the first production four-wheel-drive car in the world, designed by Major <a href="/wiki/Tony_Rolt" title="Tony Rolt">Tony Rolt</a>, and built at their factory in West Bromwich.\n</p><p>Cadbury launched <i><a href="/wiki/Cadbury_Dairy_Milk" title="Cadbury Dairy Milk">Dairy Milk</a></i> in 1905, <i><a href="/wiki/Bournville_(chocolate_bar)" title="Bournville (chocolate bar)">Bournville</a></i> in 1906, <i><a href="/wiki/Cadbury_Dairy_Milk_Fruit_%26_Nut" title="Cadbury Dairy Milk Fruit &amp; Nut">Fruit &amp; Nut</a></i> in 1928, <i>Whole Nut</i> in 1930, <i><a href="/wiki/Cadbury_Roses" title="Cadbury Roses">Cadbury Roses</a></i> in 1938, and the <i><a href="/wiki/Cadbury_Creme_Egg" title="Cadbury Creme Egg">Cadbury Creme Egg</a></i> in 1971. <a href="/wiki/George_Cadbury" title="George Cadbury">George</a> and <a href="/wiki/Richard_Cadbury" title="Richard Cadbury">Richard Cadbury</a> built their factory in 1879 and <a href="/wiki/Bournville" title="Bournville">Bournville</a> in 1893, named after the <a href="/wiki/Bournbrook" title="Bournbrook">Bourn brook</a>. <a href="/wiki/Iceland_(supermarket)" title="Iceland (supermarket)">Iceland (supermarket)</a> opened its first store in Oswestry in 1970 \xe2\x80\x93 heralding the onset of frozen food in the UK. <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Bird" title="Alfred Bird">Alfred Bird</a> invented egg-free custard in 1837 in Birmingham \xe2\x80\x93 accidentally given to guests at his home, being created as his wife had an allergy to eggs; he then invented <a href="/wiki/Baking_powder" title="Baking powder">baking powder</a> in 1843 as his wife also had an allergy to <a href="/wiki/Yeast" title="Yeast">yeast</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=West_Midlands_(region)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><a href="/wiki/J._R._R._Tolkien" title="J. R. R. Tolkien">J. R. R. Tolkien</a> grew up in Birmingham, Kings Heath, then part of Worcestershire, and was inspired by <a href="/wiki/Moseley_Bog" title="Moseley Bog">Moseley Bog</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sarehole" title="Sarehole">Sarehole</a>, and perhaps by the <a href="/wiki/Perrott%27s_Folly" title="Perrott&#39;s Folly">Perrott\'s Folly</a>. <a href="/wiki/Philip_Larkin" title="Philip Larkin">Philip Larkin</a> came from Coventry. <a href="/wiki/Rowland_Hill" title="Rowland Hill">Rowland Hill</a> (stamps) was from Kidderminster. The writer <a href="/wiki/George_Eliot" title="George Eliot">George Eliot</a> came from <a href="/wiki/Nuneaton" title="Nuneaton">Nuneaton</a>. <a href="/wiki/Anthony_E._Pratt" title="Anthony E. Pratt">Anthony E. Pratt</a> from Birmingham invented <a href="/wiki/Cluedo" title="Cluedo">Cluedo</a>.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Frederick_Gibberd" title="Frederick Gibberd">Frederick Gibberd</a> of Coventry designed <a href="/wiki/Liverpool_Metropolitan_Cathedral" title="Liverpool Metropolitan Cathedral">Liverpool Metropolitan Cathedral</a>. <a href="/wiki/Edward_Cave" title="Edward Cave">Edward Cave</a> from Rugby made Britain\'s first magazine in 1731 \xe2\x80\x93 <a href="/wiki/The_Gentleman%27s_Magazine" title="The Gentleman&#39;s Magazine">The Gentleman\'s Magazine</a>. <a href="/wiki/Philip_Astley" title="Philip Astley">Philip Astley</a> from Newcastle under Lyme invented the modern day circus in 1768 \xe2\x80\x93 <a href="/wiki/Astley%27s_Amphitheatre" title="Astley&#39;s Amphitheatre">Astley\'s Amphitheatre</a>.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Castlemorton_Common_Festival" title="Castlemorton Common Festival">Castlemorton Common Festival</a> in May 1992 near Malvern, led to the <a href="/wiki/Criminal_Justice_and_Public_Order_Act_1994" title="Criminal Justice and Public Order Act 1994">Criminal Justice and Public Order Act 1994</a>.\n</p><p>The highest numbered building in the UK is 2679 Stratford Road, <a href="/wiki/A3400_road" class="mw-redirect" title="A3400 road">Stratford Road</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hockley_Heath" title="Hockley Heath">Hockley Heath</a>.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Regional_assembly">Regional assembly</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=West_Midlands_(region)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Regional assembly">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Update" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><img alt="Ambox current red.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Ambox_current_red.svg/42px-Ambox_current_red.svg.png" width="42" height="34" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Ambox_current_red.svg/63px-Ambox_current_red.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Ambox_current_red.svg/84px-Ambox_current_red.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="360" data-file-height="290" /></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section needs to be <b>updated</b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please update this article to reflect recent events or newly available information.</span>  <small><i>(October 2014)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>The official representative body of the region is the <a href="/wiki/West_Midlands_Leaders_Board" class="mw-redirect" title="West Midlands Leaders Board">West Midlands Leaders Board</a> which has limited administrative functions such as regional planning and economic development. The board is not an elected body, but is made up of members appointed from local councils across the region and is known as a <a href="/wiki/Quango" title="Quango">quango</a>. It is based on <i>Edward Street</i> in Birmingham, near the <a href="/wiki/National_Indoor_Arena" class="mw-redirect" title="National Indoor Arena">National Indoor Arena</a>. From March 2010, the funding decisions at regional level were taken over by <a href="/wiki/Advantage_West_Midlands" title="Advantage West Midlands">Advantage West Midlands</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Regional_Development_Agency" class="mw-redirect" title="Regional Development Agency">Regional Development Agency</a>.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Demographics">Demographics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=West_Midlands_(region)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Demographics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ethnicity">Ethnicity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=West_Midlands_(region)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Ethnicity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:452px;"><a href="/wiki/File:United_Kingdom_European_Union_referendum_2016_voting_area_results_(West_Midlands).svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/United_Kingdom_European_Union_referendum_2016_voting_area_results_%28West_Midlands%29.svg/450px-United_Kingdom_European_Union_referendum_2016_voting_area_results_%28West_Midlands%29.svg.png" width="450" height="506" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/United_Kingdom_European_Union_referendum_2016_voting_area_results_%28West_Midlands%29.svg/675px-United_Kingdom_European_Union_referendum_2016_voting_area_results_%28West_Midlands%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/United_Kingdom_European_Union_referendum_2016_voting_area_results_%28West_Midlands%29.svg/900px-United_Kingdom_European_Union_referendum_2016_voting_area_results_%28West_Midlands%29.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1161" data-file-height="1306" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:United_Kingdom_European_Union_referendum_2016_voting_area_results_(West_Midlands).svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>How the West Midlands region voted in the June 2016 <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_European_Union_membership_referendum,_2016" title="United Kingdom European Union membership referendum, 2016">European Union referendum</a>; only the (university) district of Warwick voted (58.8%) to stay in the EU; the region had the highest overall vote (59.3%) to leave, with large proportions in Stoke-on-Trent (69.4%) and <a href="/wiki/Cannock_Chase_District" title="Cannock Chase District">Cannock Chase</a> (68.9%)</div></div></div>\n<p>The West Midlands is the second most ethnically diverse part of the UK (Greater London being the most diverse). This is in large part due to the <a href="/wiki/West_Midlands_conurbation" title="West Midlands conurbation">West Midlands conurbation</a>, which is highly diverse. The ethnic makeup of the West Midlands as a whole as measured by the 2011 census was as follows:\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable" style="text-align:right">\n\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>Ethnic pregnancy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=West_Midlands_(region)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Teenage pregnancy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>For top-tier authorities in the West Midlands, Stoke-on-Trent has the highest <a href="/wiki/Teenage_pregnancy_and_sexual_health_in_the_United_Kingdom" class="mw-redirect" title="Teenage pregnancy and sexual health in the United Kingdom">teenage pregnancy</a> rate. For council districts, <a href="/wiki/Nuneaton_and_Bedworth" title="Nuneaton and Bedworth">Nuneaton and Bedworth</a> in Warwickshire has the highest rate closely followed by <a href="/wiki/Tamworth,_Staffordshire" title="Tamworth, Staffordshire">Tamworth</a>. For top-tier authorities, Shropshire has the lowest rate, and for council districts <a href="/wiki/Malvern_Hills_(district)" class="mw-redirect" title="Malvern Hills (district)">Malvern Hills</a> has the lowest rate.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Social_deprivation">Social deprivation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=West_Midlands_(region)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Social deprivation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The region, from studies of multiple deprivation, shows similarities with <a href="/wiki/Yorkshire_and_the_Humber" title="Yorkshire and the Humber">Yorkshire and the Humber</a>, and is more deprived than the neighbouring East Midlands. From the <a href="/wiki/Indices_of_deprivation_2007" title="Indices of deprivation 2007">Indices of deprivation 2007</a>, it can be seen that, in common with <a href="/wiki/Northern_England" title="Northern England">Northern England</a>, the region has more <a href="/wiki/Super_Output_Areas" class="mw-redirect" title="Super Output Areas">Lower Area Super Output Areas</a> in the 20% most deprived districts than in the 20% least deprived districts.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> The region\'s most deprived council districts, in descending order, are Birmingham (10th highest in England), Sandwell (14th), Stoke-on-Trent (16th), Wolverhampton (28th), Walsall (45th), Coventry (61st), and Dudley (100th). These have Labour MPs except for a Conservative MP in Walsall, one in Sandwell, one in Dudley, and one in Wolverhampton (<a href="/wiki/Wolverhampton_South_West_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Wolverhampton South West (UK Parliament constituency)">Wolverhampton South West</a>), and a Lib Dem MP for <a href="/wiki/Birmingham_Yardley_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Birmingham Yardley (UK Parliament constituency)">Birmingham Yardley</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The least deprived districts in 2007 (before Shropshire became a unitary authority in 2009) were Bromsgrove, South Staffordshire, Warwick, Wychavon, and Lichfield. These areas, like all rural areas of the West Midlands, have Conservative MPs. At county level, the least deprived areas, in descending order, were Warwickshire, Worcestershire, Solihull, Staffordshire, and Shropshire.\n</p><p>In March 2011 the region had the second highest overall <a href="/wiki/Jobseeker%27s_Allowance" title="Jobseeker&#39;s Allowance">unemployment claimant</a> count in England at 4.7%, second to <a href="/wiki/North_East_England" title="North East England">North East England</a>. The highest in the region was Wolverhampton at 7.7%, the joint second highest (with Manchester) unemployment rate in England. Next is Sandwell with 7.1%, Birmingham with 7.0%, and Walsall with 6.4%. The lowest rate in the region is the district of <a href="/wiki/Stratford-on-Avon_(district)" class="mw-redirect" title="Stratford-on-Avon (district)">Stratford-on-Avon</a>, with 1.6% \xe2\x80\x93 one of the lowest unemployment rates in England.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Elections">Elections</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=West_Midlands_(region)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Elections">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Unreferenced" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><a href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" width="50" height="39" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="399" /></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>does not <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">cite</a> any <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">sources</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=West_Midlands_(region)&amp;action=edit">improve this section</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Burden_of_evidence" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">removed</a>.</span>  <small><i>(June 2015)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Green_Party_of_England_and_Wales" title="Green Party of England and Wales">Green Party of England and Wales</a> was formed at the Bridge Inn in <a href="/wiki/Napton-on-the-Hill" title="Napton-on-the-Hill">Napton-on-the-Hill</a>, Warwickshire, in February 1973,\nand originated from an article by <a href="/wiki/Paul_R._Ehrlich" title="Paul R. Ehrlich">Paul R. Ehrlich</a> about population growth in <i><a href="/wiki/Playboy" title="Playboy">Playboy</a></i> magazine; it became the Ecology Party in 1975 and the Green Party in 1985; the first conference was in June 1973 at <i>The Allesley Hotel</i> at the <a href="/wiki/A4114_road" class="mw-redirect" title="A4114 road">A4114</a>/A45 junction south of the main Jaguar works in <a href="/wiki/Allesley" title="Allesley">Allesley</a>, in west Coventry.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:United_Kingdom_General_Election_2017_Results_Map_(West_Midlands).svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/United_Kingdom_General_Election_2017_Results_Map_%28West_Midlands%29.svg/220px-United_Kingdom_General_Election_2017_Results_Map_%28West_Midlands%29.svg.png" width="220" height="273" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/United_Kingdom_General_Election_2017_Results_Map_%28West_Midlands%29.svg/330px-United_Kingdom_General_Election_2017_Results_Map_%28West_Midlands%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/United_Kingdom_General_Election_2017_Results_Map_%28West_Midlands%29.svg/440px-United_Kingdom_General_Election_2017_Results_Map_%28West_Midlands%29.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1089" data-file-height="1353" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:United_Kingdom_General_Election_2017_Results_Map_(West_Midlands).svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>General Election results in 2017</div></div></div>\n<p>In the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1992" title="United Kingdom general election, 1992">1992 general election</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nuneaton_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Nuneaton (UK Parliament constituency)">Nuneaton</a> was taken by Labour with a 7% swing, which was one of the largest swings of the night; in the 2015 election, the Nuneaton result would largely seal the eventual outcome of the election. In the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_2015" title="United Kingdom general election, 2015">2010 general election</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_Warwickshire_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="North Warwickshire (UK Parliament constituency)">North Warwickshire</a> (<a href="/wiki/Dan_Byles" title="Dan Byles">Dan Byles</a>) had the smallest Conservative majority, with 54; <a href="/wiki/Stoke-on-Trent_Central_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Stoke-on-Trent Central (UK Parliament constituency)">Stoke-on-Trent Central </a> had the smallest number of winning votes, with 12,220. \n</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_2015" title="United Kingdom general election, 2015">2015 general election</a>, the Conservatives gained the largest share of the region by popular vote and took control of the number of seats, with 42% of the region\'s electorate voting Conservative, 33% Labour, 16% UKIP, 6% Liberal Democrat and 3% Green. The Conservatives gained 2 seats with virtually no swing from Labour to Conservative.\n</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_2017" title="United Kingdom general election, 2017">2017 general election</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Staffordshire_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="South Staffordshire (UK Parliament constituency)">South Staffordshire</a> (<a href="/wiki/Gavin_Williamson" title="Gavin Williamson">Gavin Williamson</a>) had the second-highest Conservative vote proportion in the UK \xe2\x80\x93 69.8%. <a href="/wiki/David_Firth_(statistician)" title="David Firth (statistician)">David Firth (statistician)</a>, at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Warwick" title="University of Warwick">University of Warwick</a>, invented the BBC election <a href="/wiki/Exit_poll" title="Exit poll">exit poll</a>. 6ft 9 <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Kawczynski" title="Daniel Kawczynski">Daniel Kawczynski</a>, a Shropshire MP, is the tallest MP ever. \n</p><p>Although having a slightly smaller percentage of the vote than the neighbouring <a href="/wiki/East_Midlands" title="East Midlands">East Midlands</a>, the geographic area of the West Midlands is more Conservative, due to Labour\'s vote now consigned to the urban areas of Birmingham, Coventry and Stoke-on-Trent. The number of seats is more favoured towards Labour than the geographic spread, with 35 Conservative seats and 24 Labour. All of Warwickshire, Staffordshire (except <a href="/wiki/Newcastle-under-Lyme_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Newcastle-under-Lyme (UK Parliament constituency)">Newcastle-under-Lyme</a>), Herefordshire, Worcestershire and Shropshire is now Conservative. \n</p><p><br />\n</p>\n<div role="navigation" class="navbox" aria-labelledby="Constituencies_in_the_West_Midlands_(59)"
